        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28687687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16513758628684917543">So many people have already reviewed this book--so instead of a comprehensive review, I will only mention one of many truly memorable scenes. In a meeting of the Royal Society in London, various natural philosophers report on their recent findings, inventions and discoveries. The juxtaposition of banal reports with momentous discoveries is absolutely hilarious. I won't try to paraphrase it--the scene is lengthy--but this section is worth reading by any modern-day scientist. The point is that at </span>
  <span id="freeText16513758628684917543" style="display:none">So many people have already reviewed this book--so instead of a comprehensive review, I will only mention one of many truly memorable scenes. In a meeting of the Royal Society in London, various natural philosophers report on their recent findings, inventions and discoveries. The juxtaposition of banal reports with momentous discoveries is absolutely hilarious. I won't try to paraphrase it--the scene is lengthy--but this section is worth reading by any modern-day scientist. The point is that at the time of discovery, its true importance is not known, or even guessed at. It's only after time has passed that we learn whether a discovery is trivial or important.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771968853" />
    <a title="Lightreads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads"><img alt="Lightreads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230606449p2/1836077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771968853?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771968853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11884702592154306378">A long, meandering, Europe-trotting historical which alternates stretches of ponderous natural philosophy with stretches of hilarious piratical shenanigans, to somewhat dubious effect. I enjoyed this, the way you enjoy a book that you read in 100 page chunks over the span of a year, and it's worth noting that I could do that since there's very little throughline. But the thing is.<br/><br/>The thing is, Stephenson made a conscious choice to mix his oodles of historical research with a modern prose sensibi</span>
  <span id="freeText11884702592154306378" style="display:none">A long, meandering, Europe-trotting historical which alternates stretches of ponderous natural philosophy with stretches of hilarious piratical shenanigans, to somewhat dubious effect. I enjoyed this, the way you enjoy a book that you read in 100 page chunks over the span of a year, and it's worth noting that I could do that since there's very little throughline. But the thing is.<br /><br />The thing is, Stephenson made a conscious choice to mix his oodles of historical research with a modern prose sensibility. Which is fine, since it's not like the novel as we understand it – and as Stephenson writes it – was actually invented yet when this book is set. But his particular modern prose sensibility is basically a transcription of the stylings of an overeducated hipster douche kicking back in a hipster douche bar, telling his hipster douche friends about that time he got lost on vacation and nearly died of malaria, but the entire point of the story is to be arch and ironic and detached about the whole thing, because let's be honest, nothing bad could truly ever happen to this hipster douche, that just isn't how the world works and he knows it. I mean, he's a funny dude! He tells a great story! But there's a very fine line. <br /><br />Basically, somewhere around page 700, right about where a couple of characters were observing a rape and contemplating their imminent enslavement and death, and all they did was make arch, detached, hipster douche comments to each other about it, just as every other character had done on every other occasion from the wrenching to the banal to the sublime, right about then was when I thought <i>oh come on, a single moment of genuineness wouldn't kill you</i>. <br /><br />Except this is Stephenson, so actually . . . it might.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11884702592154306378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_43101912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43101912" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1406377-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1300409555p2/1406377.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43101912?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/1406377-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43101912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11525975136063896618">Stephenson deserves an editor that will tell him to write less. The man prodigiously describes "cool" "fun" "interesting" events with such detail and precision that it usually loses its narrative flow. The guy has a command of the english language and is certainly fascinated by late 17th century and early 18th century goings-on that this feels like a historical narrative rather than historical fiction, yet the whole book feels like it was written in computer code; it is an odd stylistic quirk of</span>
  <span id="freeText11525975136063896618" style="display:none">Stephenson deserves an editor that will tell him to write less. The man prodigiously describes "cool" "fun" "interesting" events with such detail and precision that it usually loses its narrative flow. The guy has a command of the english language and is certainly fascinated by late 17th century and early 18th century goings-on that this feels like a historical narrative rather than historical fiction, yet the whole book feels like it was written in computer code; it is an odd stylistic quirk of his that I've noticed in his past books, but does not really fit in this book. <br /><br />The most cool thing about this is that he wrote the whole book (and the next two in the trilogy - nearly three thousand full pages of book) with a fountain pen on cotton paper. Used blotting paper and everything. <br /><br />Don't get into this series unless you read quickly, REALLY like his other stuff, or are unduly fascinated with the Baroque era. I'm enjoying it, but its detractions weigh it down like a sea anchor over the course of three books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11525975136063896618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer147060173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9137715217650714348">I loved Stephenson's "Snow Crash". Really liked "Cryptonomicon". But, this novel was terribly boring. It is divided into three books. Book 1 follows the scientist Dan Waterhouse. Book 2 followed Jack Shaftoe, King of the Vagabonds. Book 3 sees Eliza, a former slave girl, caught up in a spy ring between the French, English & Dutch governments. Sounds good, but it isn't. The writing is too long, and too detailed to remain focused on what should be important to the story...the story. I found myself</span>
  <span id="freeText9137715217650714348" style="display:none">I loved Stephenson's "Snow Crash". Really liked "Cryptonomicon". But, this novel was terribly boring. It is divided into three books. Book 1 follows the scientist Dan Waterhouse. Book 2 followed Jack Shaftoe, King of the Vagabonds. Book 3 sees Eliza, a former slave girl, caught up in a spy ring between the French, English &amp; Dutch governments. Sounds good, but it isn't. The writing is too long, and too detailed to remain focused on what should be important to the story...the story. I found myself skipping paragraphs of setting and science-speak to find the plot of the novel, but to no avail. Oh, there are moments of real conflict and intrigue, but they are few and far between. I know their are two more books which will tie up loose ends, (and there are many) but I don't think I will waste my time wading through another 1800 pages (900 per book) to learn...nothing</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8643507287052937625">Neal Stephenson needs an editor.<br/><br/>Also, it may be cute and even kind of interesting to write an historical fantasy novel using idioms and vernacular from the 20th century on purpose, but it just doesn't work for me.<br/><br/>And yeah, ok we get it Neal, you're really clever and know a bunch of stuff...that doesn't mean you need to reference every bit of it you can stuff into the books you write.<br/><br/>It's kind of dissapointing because the ideas and possibilities of where this book could have been going were reall</span>
  <span id="freeText8643507287052937625" style="display:none">Neal Stephenson needs an editor.<br /><br />Also, it may be cute and even kind of interesting to write an historical fantasy novel using idioms and vernacular from the 20th century on purpose, but it just doesn't work for me.<br /><br />And yeah, ok we get it Neal, you're really clever and know a bunch of stuff...that doesn't mean you need to reference every bit of it you can stuff into the books you write.<br /><br />It's kind of dissapointing because the ideas and possibilities of where this book could have been going were really interesting...Neal Stephenson just kept getting in the way.</span>

<div id="review_370903571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/370903571" />
    <a title="Charlie Huenemann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7099752-charlie-huenemann"><img alt="Charlie Huenemann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595945579p2/7099752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/370903571?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlie Huenemann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlie Huenemann" href="/user/show/7099752-charlie-huenemann">Charlie Huenemann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer370903571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer558502343110588178">(This is a review of the whole Baroque Cycle.)<br/><br/>The saga ranges over the years 1640-1714 (roughly), following three principal characters: Daniel Waterhouse, a British natural philosopher and non-conformist; Eliza, a woman kidnapped from a remote British isle and abducted into the seraglio, who is later rescued and who subsequently makes her way into the court of Versailles and the world of high finance; and Half-Cocked Jack Shaftoe, King of the Vagabonds, adventurer, galley slave, pirate, and symp</span>
  <span id="freeText558502343110588178" style="display:none">(This is a review of the whole Baroque Cycle.)<br /><br />The saga ranges over the years 1640-1714 (roughly), following three principal characters: Daniel Waterhouse, a British natural philosopher and non-conformist; Eliza, a woman kidnapped from a remote British isle and abducted into the seraglio, who is later rescued and who subsequently makes her way into the court of Versailles and the world of high finance; and Half-Cocked Jack Shaftoe, King of the Vagabonds, adventurer, galley slave, pirate, and sympathetic everyman who will out-connive everyone unless the Imp of the Perverse compels him toward actions uncommonly glorious. Along the way we meet Newton, Leibniz, Hooke, Wren, Boyle, Locke, Peter the Great, royals of Europe, harpoon-chucking Russians, insidious Jesuits, etc., etc. and are led on great many an escapade in which, as the phrase goes, hilarity ensues.<br /><br />In some interview Stephenson defines science fiction as fiction that takes ideas seriously, and by that measure this is major league science fiction. There are no robots, cyborgs, or time travel, but we do encounter the beginnings of modern science (promiscuous with alchemy), the transmutation of rare earth metals into coins, credit, and finance, the operations of the British Mint and Royal Society, the various sultanates of Asia, the global slave trade, the manufacturing of watered steel, cryptography, the logistical difficulties of sailing and of naval warfare, the beginnings of steam engines, and the lived experience of London’s sewer problems, with lots of clanging swordplay along the way. None of these are merely glimpsed, but each is explored in such depth as to prompt the reader to wonder who the hell this Neal Stephenson person is and how many heads he might possess. For a guy who, as he says, gets paid to sit on his butt and make stuff up, the achievement of his series is jaw-dropping, and it is a ripping good yarn to boot, all 916 + 815 + 886 pages of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="558502343110588178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I don't read massive books but I did love Stephenson's cyberpunk books and this sounded interesting, but no way was I ever planning on reading it, size matters after all.<br/><br/>But I've had a hankering for something approaching the content of Wolf Hall/Bring Up the Bodies since finishing the second book and despite being set over a hundred years later - taking place after the other Cromwell was beheaded - Quicksilver is similarly furnishe</span>
  <span id="freeText14816692272683804829" style="display:none">I've owned this "cycle" of books for something like seven years. I don't read massive books but I did love Stephenson's cyberpunk books and this sounded interesting, but no way was I ever planning on reading it, size matters after all.<br /><br />But I've had a hankering for something approaching the content of Wolf Hall/Bring Up the Bodies since finishing the second book and despite being set over a hundred years later - taking place after the other Cromwell was beheaded - Quicksilver is similarly furnished with excellent period detail, political intrigues and as it's Stephenson great writing and the feeling of intense research behind it. And did I mention that it's massive?<br /><br />I don't really know why it's science fiction, perhaps the alt-hist subgenre? But I'm going with it anyway and if I'm not totally burned out after a few quick reads I want to dive right back in to the sequence and get through the next 2000 pages or whatever at least by the end of the year.</span>

<div id="review_310561084" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310561084" />
    <a title="Stephanie Swint" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8720943-stephanie-swint"><img alt="Stephanie Swint" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370546825p2/8720943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310561084?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Swint" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Swint" href="/user/show/8720943-stephanie-swint">Stephanie Swint</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8720943-stephanie-swint?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310561084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1102207804230312777">Quicksilver is an interesting book-especially since you can be discussing two different books. Quicksilver is the first installment of Neal Stephenson’s Baroque cycle. It is a political and scientific monster delving into the 17th and 18th century. The first thing you need to know is that Quicksilver:Volume One is a combination of Quicksilver:Book 1, King of the Vagabonds: Book 2, and Odalesque: Book 3. If you purchase Quicksilver Volume 1 do not purchase the Books that are available in a solita</span>
  <span id="freeText1102207804230312777" style="display:none">Quicksilver is an interesting book-especially since you can be discussing two different books. Quicksilver is the first installment of Neal Stephenson’s Baroque cycle. It is a political and scientific monster delving into the 17th and 18th century. The first thing you need to know is that Quicksilver:Volume One is a combination of Quicksilver:Book 1, King of the Vagabonds: Book 2, and Odalesque: Book 3. If you purchase Quicksilver Volume 1 do not purchase the Books that are available in a solitary format. You are not getting anything new. It is a marketing package to make the series look less intimidating. You can purchase the books in whatever format is more appealing to your reading style. My one comment is that if you choose to read Quicksilver: Book 1 rather than Volume 1 you may not be as impressed. It explores of the story of Daniel Waterhouse the friend and colleague of Isaac Newton. He is a Royal Society member in the English scientific community but ends up playing a much more political role. The scientific details are rich. Many have found them excessive. I enjoyed them but the first time I read this it was as the volume and not the book and including the stories of Half-cocked Jack and Eliza helps to make Volume holistic. I think if I had read book one initially I am not sure I would have continued. My personal recommendation, if you enjoy large dense books, read the books in the three volumes rather than the 8 book format.<br /><br />Daniel Waterhouse is a friend and colleague of Isaac Newton. He comes from a deeply religious and puritan background. His father was the infamous Drake and both paves a way for his career in Natural Philosophy as well as is a challenge. Daniel and Isaac meet in Oxford and room together. Daniel soon learns he can learn more from Isaac’s genius than what he can learn in class. The price for this is keeping Newton fed, making sure he sleeps, and taking the brunt of his moods and temper. Daniel is perhaps the only person that Isaac trusts and puts Daniel in the position of caretaker and social diplomat for Newton so the world can see his genius. Nothing would be published of Newton’s if it weren’t for Daniel because Newton. He does not produce his work for acclaim and most of his work isn’t seen by anyone else. As a result, Waterhouse does not get to pursue his own scientific queries. Instead he becomes steeped in politics and an integral important figure in the Royal Society. This is what you learn in Quicksilver: Book 1. Quicksilver: Volume 1 goes on to explore the story of Jack Shaftoe, or Half-cocked Jack. This man is not a political giant but rather an intelligent street urchin grown into a man. He’s not the best of men, but he is smart, crafty, and always seeking opportunity. Jack brings the action to this tale as his story becomes entwined with Daniel’s as well as Eliza’s. Eliza is rescued from a harem by Jack – not on purpose but their relationship is important. She is spy, financial market genius, and rescued white sex slave. If you stop at Book 1 you will miss so much. Carry on because as with all Stephenson’s books it will all tie together in the end.<br /><br />Stephenson, prior to this series, was known for his science fiction, specifically, Snow Crash and Diamond Age. These are books I love. The Baroque Cycle is purely historical and while it discusses scientific material and how it shaped 17th and 18th century society this is a historical novel. The book provides a very different view of the scientific giants of this time frame compared to the version taught to you in school. Personal issues and mental illness are evident. Stephenson is known for his level of detail and research. In this series Stephenson is even more diligent than his previous works. Later in his career Stephenson will write Mongoloid and Anathem. I am not a fan of these particular books. I have learned several things in being a fan of his work. It is not all the same and if you like one of his books it is no guarantee you will like another. You need to pick the books of his that fit in the genres you like. I enjoy his science fiction and historical fiction. I have no love for his joint works with other authors. Many of his fans have learned to pay attention to these factors. Some say this series is where his work changed for them and they lost interest. I disagree think The Baroque Cycle is intelligent and I am currently on my second read of it. You must judge for yourself, but use some of the information I presented. Approach the work in the format that agrees best with your reading style and don’t fall into the trap of purchasing a book you have already read if you purchased one of the volumes previously. I have layed out the process to reading this series below.<br /><br />THE BAROQUE CYCLE<br /><br />Volumes<br /><br />1. Quicksilver: Volume 1<br /><br />2. The Confusion: Volume 2<br /><br />3. The System of The World: Volume 3<br /><br />OR<br /><br />Books<br /><br />1. Quicksilver: Book 1<br /><br />2. King of the Vagabonds<br /><br />3. Odalisque<br /><br />4. Bonanza<br /><br />5. The Juncto<br /><br />6. Soloman’s Gold<br /><br />7. Currency<br /><br />8. The System of The World: Book</span>
  <a data-text-id="1102207804230312777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3358936087" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3358936087" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1153204-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484374549p2/1153204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3358936087?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/1153204-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Natural philosophers and their ilk</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Alan by: </span>
      Enoch the Red
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3358936087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17798152334122163434">This is how <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson' title='Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neal Stephenson</a> begins <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_' title='Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Quicksilver</a>, the first published volume of his sweeping multi-volume series, <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Baroque_Cycle' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Baroque Cycle</a>:<br/><blockquote><em>Boston Common<br/><strong>October 12, 1713, 10:33:52 A.M.</strong></em><br/>—p.3</blockquote>That time—down to the second, in a year when watches didn't even have minute hands!<br/><br/>Stephenson dispenses with such impossible precision for the rest of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_' title='Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Quicksilver</a>—the rest of its chapter headings consist of dates alone. Even so, what a wealth of information remains! Frankly, the Baroque Cycle is incredible, not just for </span>
  <span id="freeText17798152334122163434" style="display:none">This is how <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a> begins <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a>, the first published volume of his sweeping multi-volume series, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Baroque_Cycle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Baroque Cycle</a>:<br /><blockquote><em>Boston Common<br /><strong>October 12, 1713, 10:33:52 A.M.</strong></em><br />—p.3</blockquote>That time—down to the second, in a year when watches didn't even have minute hands!<br /><br />Stephenson dispenses with such impossible precision for the rest of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a>—the rest of its chapter headings consist of dates alone. Even so, what a wealth of information remains! Frankly, the Baroque Cycle is incredible, not just for its length but also for its unique combination of broad scope and minutely-observed detail. We are immediately taken there, taken in, immersed in the action, yet viewing it all with godlike clarity, cognizant of subtleties that no contemporaneous participant could have known.<br /><br />That beginning definitely makes a statement, though—speaking to a documentary eye, a sort of journalistic conceit, that Stephenson uses to excellent effect throughout these quasi-historical novels about... well, they're about <em>everything</em>, really, but mainly the Baroque Cycle chronicles the dawn of a worldview never seen before... a practical, analytical, <em>scientific</em> view of reality, that some have called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" rel="nofollow noopener">the Enlightenment</a>.<br /><br /><br />This reading begins my second sojourn through <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a>'s masterwork. I won't be analyzing every page of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> (or its successors) in depth throughout—that'd make my reactions even longer than the books, despite the fact that their three published volumes encompass more than 2,600 pages all told! But... the kind of introspection Stephenson inspires has informed my thoughts as well... so this review has, inevitably, become a long one.<br /><br />Now, you <em>could</em> go into this series cold, knowing none of the history it portrays so vividly, but be warned—<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> both displays and presupposes an encyclopaedic grasp of human knowledge. Stephenson takes for granted your own familiarity with many matters that <em>will be</em> important—and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" rel="nofollow noopener">Gottfried Leibniz</a>' public rivalry with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton" rel="nofollow noopener">Isaac Newton</a>, and its significance to mathematics, are the least of them.<br /><br /><br />Although <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> is historical fiction, it begins with a character outside history, an individual you may already remember from having read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/816.Cryptonomicon" title="Cryptonomicon by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Cryptonomicon</a> (which, by the way, I strongly recommend doing first—although the events in <u>Cryptonomicon</u> take place much more recently, Stephenson's standalone novel was written earlier, and contains much that resonates with the Baroque Cycle). Enoch Root is a redheaded stepchild—long-lived, perhaps even immortal, and accustomed to being a fly on the wall of scientific history, one whose sharp eye is focused on the moment when natural philosophy transmuted from alchemy into systematic inquiry. Enoch the Red is more than an observer, though—Stephenson portrays Enoch as an <em>agent</em> of history, nudging and tweaking events in a way that sometimes seems <em>unnecessary</em>, akin to the demeaning role of so-called "Ancient Astronauts" in some versions of pseudohistory.<br /><br />But Enoch's appearances in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> are relatively few. Most of this volume is concerned with characters who are <em>real</em> (or at least unquestionably <em>realistic</em>) historical figures—like Daniel Waterhouse, a likeable fellow for an ex-Puritan, who early in the book muses (and with good reason) that<blockquote>One cannot board a ship without imagining ship-wreck.<br />—p.77</blockquote>This put me in mind of the time—once, long ago—when I myself was sitting on an airplane preparing for takeoff, and experienced a vivid, detailed premonition of just how the plane was going to crash... heeling over at the end of the runway, pinwheeling on its left wing and bursting into flame, killing all aboard. Strapped into my seat, I considered leaving the plane, but... they'd already closed the door, and I suppressed my worries.<br /><br />Of course, the plane took off without incident, and brought me to my destination as expected—but I remember that premonition to this day, and for those few moments I believe I knew <em>exactly</em> what Daniel Waterhouse was feeling.<br /><br />The same is true of this next quote as well:<blockquote>Crying <em>loudly</em> is childish, in that it reflects a belief, on the cryer's part, that someone is around to hear the noise, and come a-running to make it all better. Crying in absolute silence, as Daniel does this morning, is the mark of the mature sufferer who no longer nurses, nor is nursed by, any such comfortable delusions.<br />—p.162</blockquote><br /><br />Unavoidably, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> is informed by Stephenson's vantage point in modernity—tho' it uses archaic constructions like "phant'sy" to give a period flavor, it's clear throughout that his <em>perspective</em> is thoroughly modern. This sometimes leads to anachronisms—see whether you can spot the one in this passage, for example:<br /><blockquote>"Chapter 10 is where Wilkins explains steganography, or how to embed a subliminal message in an innocuous-seeming letter—"<br />—Daniel Waterhouse, p.206</blockquote>I was surprised to learn that the word <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steganography" rel="nofollow noopener">steganography</a> was <em>not</em> anachronistic—the term was actually first used in 1499 A.D. (!)—whereas "subliminal" turns out to be a 19th-Century coinage.<br /><br />Stephenson does address these moments out of time directly later on, by the way, in an interview appended to the trade-paperback edition of the <em>next</em> book in the Cycle, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a> (which I am, somewhat anachronistically, including here):<br /><blockquote>I never tried to entertain the illusion that I was going to write something that had no trace of the twentieth century or the twenty-first century in it. It's a given that a book is going to reflect the time in which it is written. I didn't feel a strong compulsion to avoid such anachronisms, and if something came up that I thought might be funny, or that might work, I would just go ahead and slap it in there.<br />—from "A Discussion with Neal Stephenson," p.3 of the end matter from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/822.The_Confusion__The_Baroque_Cycle___2_" title="The Confusion (The Baroque Cycle, #2) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Confusion</a></blockquote><br /><br />Stephenson also mentions several times that the word "CABAL" was supposed to stand for the initials of specific Lords. This is incorrect, but it's not an error on his part—this conspiracy theory about the CABAL was a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cabal#Etymology" rel="nofollow noopener">common belief at the time</a>.<br /><br /><br />I may be focusing a little too much on the historicity of Stephenson's series anyway, though—when in fact it's the swashbuckling <em>adventure</em> that makes this Cycle so entertaining. The proceedings of the Royal Society may be of more significance to history, but it's the romance between Half-Cocked Jack Shaftoe, King of the Vagabonds, and the sharp-witted and thoroughly mesmerizing Eliza that really makes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> come alive. After all,<blockquote>No man was more comprehensively doomed than him whose chief source of gratification was making favorable impressions on some particular woman.<br />—Jack Shaftoe, p.389</blockquote>And, from later on,<blockquote>"Because, Jack, you volunteered to be taken down into eternal torment in place of her. This is the absolute <em>minimum</em> (unless I'm mistaken) that any female requires from her man."<br />—Enoch Root, p.464</blockquote>In short, Jack and Eliza's wanderings are <em>fun</em>, in a much more straightforward way than the intricate clockwork machinations of Daniel Waterhouse and the Royal Society.<br /><br />Those machinations do convey their own sort of enjoyment, though...<blockquote>In essence the mint was a brute with a great big hammer and a punch. He was supplied with blank disks of silver—these were not money—put the punch on each one and bashed it with the hammer, mashing the portrait of some important hag, and some incantations in Latin, into it—at which point it <em>was</em> money. Officials, supervisors, assayers, clerks, guards, and, in general, the usual crowd of parasitical gentlefolk clustered around the brute with the hammer, but like lice on an ox they could not conceal the simple nature of the beast.<br />—p.415</blockquote>We may have gotten rid of the brute with the hammer (for the most part), but economics <em>still</em> seems to be a discipline devoted to obscuring the simple, rather than to simplifying the obscure.<br /><br />Speaking of obscurity... <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a> is also a devotee of obscure vocabulary—I have now become especially enamored of the word "<a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/obnubilate" rel="nofollow noopener">obnubilated</a>" (as used on p.480 <em>et seq.</em>). Its meaning may be obvious from context, but "obnubilated" is a word that I had <em>never</em> encountered before seeing it in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a>.<br /><br />Stephenson's also familiar with, and willing to cite, much of the so-called Western canon. Passages from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost by John Milton" rel="nofollow noopener">Paradise Lost</a> appear as epigrams atop many chapters, for example. Other recurring literary touchstones include <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16244.John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan" rel="nofollow noopener">John Bunyan</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29797.The_Pilgrim_s_Progress" title="The Pilgrim's Progress by John Bunyan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pilgrim's Progress</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10122.Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes" rel="nofollow noopener">Thomas Hobbes</a>' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91953.Leviathan" title="Leviathan by Thomas Hobbes" rel="nofollow noopener">Leviathan</a>. This quote from Hobbes, for example, is as apposite today as it was in the 17th Century:<blockquote><em>Therefore it happeneth commonly, that such as value themselves by the greatness of their wealth, adventure on crimes, upon hope of escaping punishment, by corrupting public justice, or obtaining pardon by money or other rewards.</em><br />—p.793.</blockquote><br /><br />Which is not to say that Stephenson can't manage his <em>own</em> prose, and very well indeed. Waxing poetical after an interval of jolly rogering (heh), for example, we encounter this bit of scene-setting:<blockquote>The Duke of Monmouth sighed and slammed back into the mattress, driving out an evoluting cloud of dust, straw-ends, bedbugs and mite foeces. All of it hung beautifully in the lambent air, as if daubed on canvas by one of those Brueghels.<br />—p.546</blockquote><br />Or consider this observation:<blockquote>"But I have observed that the best people are frequently odd in one way or another. I have got in the habit of seeking them out, and declining to trust anyone who has no oddities."<br />—William of Orange, to Eliza, p.746</blockquote><br /><br /><br />At the end, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a> contains some ten pages of <em>Dramatis Personae</em>—which in this case is where such information <em>belongs</em>, because knowing the players in advance reveals too much about the progress of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823.Quicksilver__The_Baroque_Cycle___1_" title="Quicksilver (The Baroque Cycle, #1) by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksilver</a>, information that one might not want to know in detail before embarking on the journey.<br /><br />I have myself tried to avoid spoilers in this review, and if I have revealed too much herein, let it be understood that there is, even so, much more to be discovered in the <u>Baroque Cycle</u>. I have but scratched the surface...<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17798152334122163434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119832217" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1918165735483721221">I’m a scientist by profession and I love history. Thus, I’m fascinated by the history of science, especially the era of Isaac Newton et al. So, Neal Stephenson’s Quicksilver should be just my thing and I was fully expecting to love this book (it’s been on my list for years), but I’m sad to say that I was disappointed in this first installment of The Baroque Cycle, though I still have high hopes for the remaining books.<br/><br/>Quicksilver is well-researched and well-written and chock full of plenty of st</span>
  <span id="freeText1918165735483721221" style="display:none">I’m a scientist by profession and I love history. Thus, I’m fascinated by the history of science, especially the era of Isaac Newton et al. So, Neal Stephenson’s Quicksilver should be just my thing and I was fully expecting to love this book (it’s been on my list for years), but I’m sad to say that I was disappointed in this first installment of The Baroque Cycle, though I still have high hopes for the remaining books.<br /><br />Quicksilver is well-researched and well-written and chock full of plenty of stuff I love to read about: 17th and 18th century scholars and politicians exploring the way the world works.               <span id="reviewTextContainer39977876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6612542659239393047">I admit it. I have absolutely no desire to finish this book. I'm so very close to the end, but I stopped caring somewhere along the way. I really don't know what it is that keeps me from finishing it. Maybe it's because I only have about 120 pages left and I know that nothing's going to be resolved. Maybe it's because I've read 781 pages and have no idea what the heck is going on. Is there even a plot? Is this book about anything other than history? I can see why people do like it. In fact, I li</span>
  <span id="freeText6612542659239393047" style="display:none">I admit it. I have absolutely no desire to finish this book. I'm so very close to the end, but I stopped caring somewhere along the way. I really don't know what it is that keeps me from finishing it. Maybe it's because I only have about 120 pages left and I know that nothing's going to be resolved. Maybe it's because I've read 781 pages and have no idea what the heck is going on. Is there even a plot? Is this book about anything other than history? I can see why people do like it. In fact, I like it myself. I just can't bring myself to pick it up again.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UCDJaW9iLkBZ1weIDFTn1rVOpK5t6009LRGVHvnxWMdqBqC71DznKP78s+n7oe0JYxFDZqNmw1p2b9OvNLKqXQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vIxqr1uwoxF92XCiKc7SBesnOaGIlrctq3RyYaR8xHqGqgN94O5qedryxMPeO9jaPXjeaUYbOUrwCjTQaT824A==')})
  }
//]]>

An entire subculture of p<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5316248603683733288\" style=\"display:none\">Cayce Pollard is an expensive, spookily intuitive market-research consultant. In London on a job, she is offered a secret assignment: to investigate some intriguing snippets of video that have been appearing on the Internet. An entire subculture of people is obsessed with these bits of footage, and anybody who can create that kind of brand loyalty would be a gold mine for Cayce\'s client. But when her borrowed apartment is burgled and her computer hacked, she realizes there\'s more to this project than she had expected.\n\nStill, Cayce is her father\'s daughter, and the danger makes her stubborn. Win Pollard, ex-security expert, probably ex-CIA, took a taxi in the direction of the World Trade Center on September 11 one year ago, and is presumed dead. Win taught Cayce a bit about the way agents work. She is still numb at his loss, and, as much for him as for any other reason, she refuses to give up this newly weird job, which will take her to Tokyo and on to Russia. With help and betrayal from equally unlikely quarters, Cayce will follow the trail of the mysterious film to its source, and in the process will learn something about her father\'s life and This book charts its growth and development by sampling from the work of some of its foremost practitioners, beginning with Isaac Newton and Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz in the late seventeenth century and continuing to Henri Lebesgue at the dawn of the twentieth--mathematicians whose achievements are comparable to those of Bach in music or Shakespeare in literature. William Dunham lucidly presents the definitions, theorems, and proofs. Students of literature read Shakespeare; students of music listen to Bach, he writes. But this tradition of studying the major works of the masters is, if not wholly absent, certainly uncommon in mathematics. This book seeks to redress that situation.\n\n Like a great museum, The Calculus Gallery is filled with masterpieces, among which are Bernoulli\'s early attack upon the harmonic series (1689), Euler\'s brilliant approximation of pi (1779), Cauchy\'s classic proof of the fundamental theorem of calculus (1823), Weierstrass\'s mind-boggling counterexample (1872), and Baire\'s original category theorem (1899). Collectively, these selections document the evolution of calculus from a powerful but logically chaotic subject into one whose foundations are thorough, rigorous, and unflinching--a story of genius triumphing over some of the toughest, most subtle problems imaginable.\n\n Anyone who has studied and enjoyed calculus will discover in these pages the sheer excitement each mathematician must have felt when pushing into the unknown. After reluctantly leaving his family in Boston, Waterhouse arrives in England and is almost killed by a mysterious Infernal Device. Having been away from the war-decimated country for two decades, Waterhouse quickly learns that although many things have changed, there is still violent revolution simmering just beneath the surface of seemingly civilized society. With Queen Anne deathly ill and Tories and Whigs jostling for political supremacy, Waterhouse and Newton vow to figure out who is trying to kill certain scientists and decipher the riddle behind the legend of King Solomon\'s gold, a mythical hoard of precious metal with miraculous properties.\n\nArguably one of the most ambitious -- and most researched -- stories ever written, Stephenson\'s Baroque Cycle is set in one of the most turbulent and exciting times in human history. Filled with wild adventure, political intrigue, social upheaval, civilization-changing discoveries, cabalistic mysticism, and even a little romance, this massive saga is worth its weight in
